Special Item

Mr. Fei Tang's Report 273

Abstract of Translated Sections from the First to Third Volumes (3)

Chapter 5 continues to describe how the Shanghai Municipal Council (工部局) obtained recognition for its demands to define and restrict the actions of Chinese authorities within the International Settlement, thus gradually implementing its functions and establishing a system of rule of law within the Settlement. The key points regarding the establishment of its demands are summarized into four categories as follows: (1) Courts: In 1864, the Mixed Court (會審公廨) was first established in the International Settlement. A judge appointed by the Daotai (道台) and an assessor appointed by the consuls of the treaty powers jointly tried cases. Subsequently, the court was recognized, within certain limits, to exercise its criminal and civil jurisdiction over Chinese residents and foreign nationals without extraterritorial rights within the Settlement. Before the establishment of the Mixed Court, all Chinese residents in the Settlement who committed crimes were handed over to Chinese officials outside the Settlement for interrogation. After its establishment, the exercise of judicial power by Chinese authorities within the International Settlement was restricted, and the punishment of criminals and judicial administration within the Settlement became more suitable to the needs of the Settlement. (2) Police: In order to prevent yamen runners from the Chinese city from arresting Chinese residents within the Settlement, the Shanghai Municipal Council (工部局) had to demand exclusive police power within the International Settlement. Later, it sought to obtain further demands, that is, Chinese residents arrested by the Shanghai Municipal Council (工部局) police should be pre-tried by the Mixed Court before being handed over to officials outside the Settlement, so as to ascertain whether the arrested person had a prima facie case of crime. (3) Taxes: The difficulties arising from the collection of various taxes within the International Settlement in the early days by the Chinese authorities are described. The gradual achievement of the Council's demands, such as the prohibition of establishing likin bureaus (釐局) within the Settlement, the prohibition of likin officers from operating within the Settlement, and the final recognition of the principle that Chinese officials shall not collect other taxes in the Settlement other than customs duties and land taxes. It is also traced in the report that unapproved taxes, such as collection by tax collectors within the Settlement, and punishment when detected, etc. (4) Legislation: The Shanghai Daotai and the judges of the Mixed Court often issued proclamations within the International Settlement intending to give them legal effect. How the habit of issuing them is described in this chapter. How the Council opposed this type of proclamation is also explained. The provisions in the proclamation sometimes interfered with the property rights and business rights of residents within the International Settlement. The matter was discussed by both sides. The final result was that the proclamations of the Shanghai Daotai or the judges of the Mixed Court could not be posted in the International Settlement without the consent of the consular body and the Council. How the Council's opinions on these various issues and the truth about the fundamental issues involved can be expressed by modern official documents is cited in the report. The following is its commentary. As far as specific events are concerned, it is not necessarily certain whether the Council's observation of facts or its argument for the interpretation of treaties is accurate, but the difficulties it encountered were real and important. This is not likely to be wrong. The Council has been working hard to build a suitable system of governance, partly based on customs beyond the letter of the treaties, on the grounds that the treaties (including the Land Regulations) must be applied. However, if the treaties are not supplemented by customs in accordance with the circumstances at the time, the wording of the treaties will be full of obstacles and difficult to implement, and the jurisdiction over the Settlement proposed in the treaties will be of no value. The Chinese authorities' use of pressure to establish its demands by the Council is often regarded as a means of foreign invasion, or as a part of what is now called "foreign imperialism." Foreign consuls' insistence on protecting the leaders of foreign communities inside and outside the Settlement sometimes seems to be merely a selfish desire to protect their private interests, the most prominent being the landowning interests of foreigners inside and outside the Settlement. However, from the perspective of the Council, which is truly responsible for governance, the issue at hand has a direct impact not only on the welfare of tax-paying Chinese and residents within the Settlement, but also on the interests of foreigners and the prosperity and progress of the entire International Settlement. The Council's declaration states that the directors of the Council believe that if their main point of contention, namely that the exercise of authority by Chinese officials over Chinese residents within the Settlement must be restricted, cannot be achieved, then the Council will be unable to implement an effective system of governance within the International Settlement in accordance with the Land Regulations, and there may be even greater hidden disputes in the overall situation. In essence, the difficulty that the Council seeks to resolve is not merely a conflict between two governing bodies, but a conflict between two governing principles. Within this conflict, the International Settlement governing body represents the limited powers exercised under the control of the courts. If the unlimited powers exercised by the Chinese authorities within the International Settlement are not restricted, then the limited powers will inevitably be overthrown. If this fact can be recognized, then the resentment aroused by past conflicts among the Chinese people, a part of which still harbors resentment and is sufficient to influence political action, may be calmed. In fact, the current Chinese government has already established a goal to include the governing principles that foreigners, whether they understand them or not, have always strived for in the national constitution. What is this principle? It is to establish the rule of law as the only effective way to maintain individual freedom and protect civil rights.

Chapter 6 274

Traces the history of the issue of Chinese representation in the Shanghai Municipal Council (工部局) of the International Settlement. When the Land Regulations proposed in 1866 were discussed, the proposal to elect Chinese representatives was discussed. At that time, the consular body also drafted a method proposing that representatives and leaders of various Chinese organizations elect three Chinese representatives to form an advisory committee to consult with the Council on matters relating to Chinese residents. The Chairman of the Council once sent a letter stating his opinion on this proposed method. This letter was published in December 1866. The Council's acceptance of Chinese representatives is an innovation that may cause differences of opinion, but it does not seem to have adverse results. If it can improve the friendship between China and foreign countries, the benefits will be great. Knowing the opinions of the Chinese on Chinese administrative orders is beneficial to foreigners. On the Chinese side, they can gain true knowledge of the free representative system prevailing in our country and the fair judicial administration that we value. The above-mentioned method of establishing a Chinese advisory committee was not included in the Land Regulations finally approved by the ministers of various countries in Beijing in 1869. The reason for this is not known. The formal re-mention of the issue of Chinese representation was delayed until nearly 40 years later. In December 1905, after the Mixed Court incident, Chinese business leaders cooperated with the Council to discuss the necessary measures to restore the original state. The result was a proposal to establish a recognition mechanism for future consultations between the Council and Chinese business leaders. This proposal can be found in a letter from Mr. Yu Qiaqing to the Chairman of the Council in February 1906. The letter stated that the Chinese have held a meeting, and all directors of the Shanghai business associations have attended and unanimously agreed to elect seven people to form a Chinese Chamber of Commerce to represent the interests of various industries. Later, a letter was sent clarifying that the name of the committee was the "Representative Committee." Officials do not join and do not participate in the election. The duties of the committee are to propose various suggestions to improve the welfare of the International Settlement. If existing regulations and rules relating to Chinese interests are to be changed, the Council is expected to give the committee an opportunity to consult first. The letter also stated that the purpose of establishing the committee is to enable the Council to know the opinions and feelings of Chinese groups on issues of particular interest to the Chinese, and to make the committee a recognition mechanism for Chinese residents to convey complaints to the Council, and to enhance credit and goodwill between the Chinese and foreign people and promote the welfare and progress of the International Settlement. The Council welcomed the above proposal, but this aroused criticism from the foreign press. In addition to other opposition points, the reason for the criticism from the foreign press was that the proposed committee would seek to confront the Council and was a dangerous thing. The committee was in a semi-official position and was produced through formal election procedures. During the recent incident, Chinese officials' attitude of showing hatred towards the Council may be used to secretly plot to undermine the Council's power, which is frightening. However, some commentators strongly urged the election of two Chinese to join the board of directors, believing that using this method to know the opinions of the Chinese at any time is better than using other methods. In March 1906, Western taxpayers continued to hold an annual meeting and passed a proposal denying the Council the right to recognize the proposed establishment of the committee, on the grounds that the Council did not have this right according to the Land Regulations. The following commentary was made on the rejection of the proposed establishment of the Chinese committee at that time. According to the official documents listed above, the attempt to establish a mechanism for the Council to contact Chinese groups in the Settlement did not produce results. This incident is unpleasant to read, because the Western taxpayers' opposition led to the loss of a great opportunity. This is an undeniable conclusion. This opportunity would have allowed the development of the International Settlement system to take a step further, and after a period of time, a significant effect could have occurred to make the relationship between the Council and foreign communities and Chinese residents in the Settlement more satisfying and sound. There may have been sufficient reasons to treat the proposed method with caution, but the Chinese groups clearly initiated this motion out of friendship, and the Council had already made such progress in approaching the Chinese, but the Western taxpayers' annual meeting completely rejected it, which was a major misfortune. The suggestion was initiated by the Chinese and suddenly frustrated, and its impact seems to have been enough to prevent the Chinese from continuing to propose the original proposal for many years, and enough to alienate the Chinese from the Council and not support it. In the following 20 years, the Council had extremely important issues to deal with. If it could have obtained the assistance of the Chinese, the benefits would have been extremely great. The draft treaty for the extension of the International Settlement was passed by the taxpayers' annual meeting in March 1915, one of the conditions of which was to propose the establishment of a Chinese advisory council, but because the draft treaty was not approved, the proposal could not become a reality. The original proposal was revived in 1920 and the taxpayers' annual meeting passed the establishment of a Chinese advisory committee. The committee was established in May 1921. All of these points are mentioned in the report. The report indicates that after the May Thirtieth Movement in 1925, the issue of Chinese representation took on a new aspect. The resignation of the Chinese advisory committee was one of the results of the incident. On April 14, 1926, a motion was put forward at the taxpayers' annual meeting in favor of accommodating Chinese representatives. The motion was passed by the meeting and it was subsequently decided to set up three Chinese directors and six Chinese committee members, which was implemented in April 1928. The report also mentioned that the number of Chinese directors was increased from three to five in May 1930.

Chapter 7 275

Discusses the historical position of the Chinese in the administration of the International Settlement with regard to paying taxes and sharing the benefits of municipal services within the Settlement. The question is why the former Council had no Chinese representatives and why there were no regulations to ensure that the Council's policies and administrative methods were subject to a systematic consultation between the Council and representatives recognized as representing the Chinese residents in the Settlement. Although the Council had no regular contact with Chinese public opinion representatives, was the Council fair in its treatment of Chinese interests? The increase in the number of Chinese residents in the International Settlement is discussed as follows: Since 1876, the number of Chinese residents in the International Settlement has gradually increased, which shows that most Chinese believe that the situation within the Settlement is better than that outside the Settlement. The reason why they believe it is better is probably because the International Settlement is the center of commerce and industry and has economic attractiveness. There is no doubt about this. However, the economic situation is closely related to the administrative situation. The continuous migration of Chinese to the International Settlement can also prove that they know that the Settlement system is beneficial. The nature of this benefit is very real. The previous chapter has described that the Council has adopted various methods to protect the interests of the Chinese from the arbitrary actions of officials outside the Settlement. The gradual expansion of Chinese commerce within the International Settlement is also a very important reason for this protection. The outstanding achievements of municipal management in the International Settlement have resulted in a great improvement in the living conditions within the Settlement, and most Chinese also enjoy it. The Chinese must pay taxes on time, but the tax rates are fixed and the benefits of compensation are obvious. The methods used by Chinese officials in various districts outside the Settlement to levy and collect taxes are lax and irregular, so that the taxes levied are not fixed and are often arbitrary and prone to harshness. For ambitious merchants, the tax is even more harsh because their business is smooth and their financial resources are abundant. After paying the tax, there is no guarantee of what compensation benefits will be received. The taxes collected locally are used to provide public services for the direct benefit of the taxpayers, rather than for the benefit of the collecting officials or their subordinates, or the tax-contracting agencies. However, the Chinese administrative authorities had little understanding of this view until recently. The excellent road administration and effective police services in the International Settlement clearly demonstrate how the taxes collected by the Council are used. The report indicates that the issue to be considered in the title of this chapter is not so much whether the Chinese in the Settlement are better off under the rule of the Settlement than if they were ruled by Chinese officials outside the Settlement, but rather whether the administration of the Settlement, on the whole, treats the Chinese and foreigners fairly.

Weather Report 276

The Xujiahui Observatory's weather forecast at 4:30 PM yesterday stated that the weather in the Yangtze River basin is unstable and very warm, with heavy fog or thunderstorms. Due to the passage of low pressure, this area and the coastal area are moving northeast-east or northeast, so there are strong gusty cyclones. The Shandong coast has strong northerly winds. South of Ma'anshan Island, the wind is variable. Also, in the 24 hours before 3:00 PM yesterday, the highest temperature in this city was 82.6 and the lowest was 53.8. On the same day last year, the highest was 67.8 and the lowest was 45.0.
